import torch
import torch.nn as nn

# U-Net Implementation
class DoubleConv(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels):
        super().__init__()
        self.double_conv = nn.Sequential(
            nn.Conv2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels),
            nn.Softmax(dim=-1),
            nn.Conv2d(out_channels, out_channels, kernel_size=3, padding=1),
            nn.BatchNorm2d(out_channels),
            nn.Softmax(dim=-1)
        )

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.double_conv(x)

class Model(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, features):
        """
        :param in_channels: Number of input channels
        :param out_channels: Number of output channels
        :param features: Number of base features (will be doubled in each layer)
        """
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.encoder1 = DoubleConv(in_channels, features)
        self.pool1 = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.encoder2 = DoubleConv(features, features * 2)
        self.pool2 = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.encoder3 = DoubleConv(features * 2, features * 4)
        self.pool3 = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.encoder4 = DoubleConv(features * 4, features * 8)
        self.pool4 = nn.MaxPool2d(kernel_size=2, stride=2)

        self.bottleneck = DoubleConv(features * 8, features * 16)

        self.upconv4 = nn.ConvTranspose2d(features * 16, features * 8, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.decoder4 = DoubleConv(features * 16, features * 8)
        self.upconv3 = nn.ConvTranspose2d(features * 8, features * 4, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.decoder3 = DoubleConv(features * 8, features * 4)
        self.upconv2 = nn.ConvTranspose2d(features * 4, features * 2, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.decoder2 = DoubleConv(features * 4, features * 2)
        self.upconv1 = nn.ConvTranspose2d(features * 2, features, kernel_size=2, stride=2)
        self.decoder1 = DoubleConv(features * 2, features)

        self.final_conv = nn.Conv2d(features, out_channels, kernel_size=1)

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        :param x: Input tensor, shape (batch_size, in_channels, height, width)
        :return: Output tensor, shape (batch_size, out_channels, height, width)
        """
        enc1 = self.encoder1(x)
        enc2 = self.encoder2(self.pool1(enc1))
        enc3 = self.encoder3(self.pool2(enc2))
        enc4 = self.encoder4(self.pool3(enc3))

        bottleneck = self.bottleneck(self.pool4(enc4))

        dec4 = self.upconv4(bottleneck)
        dec4 = torch.cat((dec4, enc4), dim=1)
        dec4 = self.decoder4(dec4)
        dec3 = self.upconv3(dec4)
        dec3 = torch.cat((dec3, enc3), dim=1)
        dec3 = self.decoder3(dec3)
        dec2 = self.upconv2(dec3)
        dec2 = torch.cat((dec2, enc2), dim=1)
        dec2 = self.decoder2(dec2)
        dec1 = self.upconv1(dec2)
        dec1 = torch.cat((dec1, enc1), dim=1)
        dec1 = self.decoder1(dec1)

        return self.final_conv(dec1)
    
batch_size = 8
in_channels = 8
out_channels = 4
height = 64
width = 512
features = 64
# Test code for UNet
def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, features]

This is a large model, with 4 encoder blocks (DoubleConv: conv + BN + softmax + conv + BN + softmax), 4 max pools, bottleneck, 4 upconv transposed convs, 4 decoder DoubleConvs, concatenations, and final 1x1 conv. We need to produce numerically equivalent outputs, within fp32 tolerance. The activation is Softmax(dim=-1) here. Note: Softmax along dim=-1 for a 4D tensor (N,C,H,W) with dim=-1 means along the W dimension (since last dimension is width). In the reference DoubleConv, they apply nn.Softmax(dim=-1) after each BatchNorm. That's unusual for a UNet: they apply softmax across W. We must replicate this exactly. The challenge is to write a single fused kernel or a few kernels that replaces the entire forward. Since the model has many layers and large channel counts, fusing everything into a single kernel might be too large and cause register spilling. But the evaluator likely expects us to fuse as much as possible. However, the problem statement says "replace the forward computation... with a custom CUDA kernel". That implies we can write one big kernel that computes the entire forward pass. But the kernel would need to read the input and produce the output, using all the weights and biases. However, there are many parameters. We would need to store all weights in some format. Alternatively, we can use a few custom kernels for each block, but the main speedup likely comes from fusing the elementwise operations (BN + softmax) with convolutions. Upconv2: ConvTranspose2d(256->128, k=2,s=2) -> [8,128,32,256] Concat(dec2, enc2) -> [8,256,32,256] Decoder2: DoubleConv(256->128) Upconv1: ConvTranspose2d(128->64, k=2,s=2) -> [8,64,64,512] Concat(dec1, enc1) -> [8,128,64,512] Decoder1: DoubleConv(128->64) Final conv: 64->4, k=1 -> [8,4,64,512] Total operations: convolutions dominate. Since dimensions are moderate and channels go up to 1024, cuDNN will be quite efficient. If we write a custom kernel that does 3x3 conv + BN + softmax(dim=-1), the key insight is: - For each output channel c, height h, and batch b, we need to compute all 512 width values - Then compute BN per channel - Then softmax over width For a tile approach: a thread block could process, say, 4 channels × 1 height × 1 batch, and all 512 width values. But 512 width with 3x3 conv means for each output pixel at width w, we need inputs at w-1, w, w+1. So for each channel and height, we process all width values. For a 3x3 conv with padding=1, the computation for a row is essentially 3 parallel dot products with shifts. For a single output channel, processing all width values for one input channel tile: - Load input row (h-1 to h+1) × width into shared memory - Each thread computes conv for one (or multiple) width positions - To be efficient, we'd want coalesced memory access along width If we have 512 width positions and use 512 threads, each thread can compute one width position. But we need 3×3 neighborhood. For channel tiling: we can accumulate over input channels in tiles. Say 32 input channels at a time: - Load 3 rows × 32 channels into shared memory - Each of 512 threads computes its (b, c_out, h, w) partial sum by iterating over the 32 input channels and 3×3 kernel - Iterate over input channel tiles This is a standard implicit GEMM / shared memory conv approach. For output, after accumulating the conv result, we apply BN and softmax in a cooperative manner within the thread block. Let me think about dimensions for a DoubleConv kernel: Input: [B, C_in, H, W] Output: [B, C_out, H, W] First conv: C_in -> C_out, 3x3 BN on C_out Softmax on W Second conv: C_out -> C_out, 3x3 BN on C_out Softmax on W For encoder1: [8, 8, 64, 512] -> [8, 64, 64, 512] C_in = 8, C_out = 64 A thread block layout: - BlockDim.x = 128 or 256 threads (along width) - BlockDim.y = some height positions - Process one or more batches and output channels Actually, a common approach is to have each thread block responsible for a tile of (B × C_out × H) with all W. For encoder1: - B=8, C_out=64, H=64, W=512 - A thread block could handle 1 batch × 1 output channel × some height positions × all width If block handles 4 height positions: 4 × 512 = 2048 output pixels. With 256 threads, each thread handles 8 width positions across 4 rows = 32 pixels total. That's a reasonable workload. But for the bottleneck: - B=8, C_out=1024, H=4, W=32 - A thread block handling 1 batch × 1 output channel × 4 height × 32 width = 128 pixels - With 128 threads, each thread does 1 pixel. This seems feasible. For transpose conv (upconv): - ConvTranspose2d(features*16, features*8, 2x2, stride=2) on [8, 1024, 4, 32] This produces [8, 512, 8, 64] ConvTranspose2d with kernel=2, stride=2 essentially doubles spatial dimensions. For output at position (h, w), it depends on input at (h//2, w//2) with kernel offset (h%2, w%2). This is actually a strided conv, which can be implemented efficiently. This is getting extremely complex to write all in one kernel. Maybe we should write multiple kernels, one per major block. Let me think about how to structure the code. We can write a generic kernel template for: 1. Conv2d + BN + Softmax 2. MaxPool2d 3. ConvTranspose2d + BN + Softmax 4. Conv2d + BN + Softmax (with concat) Actually, maybe a better approach is to recognize that for a U-Net, we can write the forward pass as a sequence of custom kernels, each fusing as much as possible: Kernel 1: Fused conv + BN + softmax (for encoders and bottleneck) Kernel 2: MaxPool2d Kernel 3: Fused convTranspose + cat + conv + BN + softmax (for decoders) But even this is a lot to implement. And the question is whether we can beat cuDNN by fusing. Hmm, actually, I just realized something. PyTorch normally uses cuDNN for conv + batch_norm fusion when possible, but it CANNOT fuse with softmax because softmax is a non-linear reduction across the width dimension. So the reference probably does: 1. Conv (cuDNN, fast) 2. BN (fused or separate, fast) 3. Softmax (separate kernel, memory bound) 4. Conv (cuDNN, fast) 5. BN (fused or separate, fast) 6. Softmax (separate kernel, memory bound) And also many memory allocations for intermediates. If we fuse the entire DoubleConv into one kernel: conv1 + bn1 + softmax1 + conv2 + bn2 + softmax2, we eliminate several round-trips to global memory. For encoder1: - Input: [8, 8, 64, 512] (8MB) - After conv1: [8, 64, 64, 512] (64MB) - no need to write to global memory! - After bn1+softmax1: [8, 64, 64, 512] (64MB) - no need to write! - After conv2: [8, 64, 64, 512] (64MB) - no need to write! - After bn2+softmax2: [8, 64, 64, 512] (64MB) - only write output! Instead of: Read 8MB -> Compute conv1 -> Write 64MB -> Read 64MB -> BN -> Write 64MB -> Read 64MB -> Softmax -> Write 64MB -> Read 64MB -> Conv2 -> Write 64MB -> Read 64MB -> BN -> Write 64MB -> Read 64MB -> Softmax -> Write 64MB That's potentially saving reading/writing ~320MB of intermediate data per encoder1! For 3x3 conv, the "direct" approach in shared memory can actually be quite efficient: - Each thread block computes a tile of output. - We load a tile of input into shared memory (with halo). - Then compute the conv by iterating over input channels. Let me design a 3x3 conv kernel: For a tile of (TILE_H, TILE_W) output pixels, we need (TILE_H+2, TILE_W+2) input pixels. Total shared memory for input tile: (TILE_H+2) * (TILE_W+2) * sizeof(float) For TILE_H=16, TILE_W=64: 18 * 66 * 4 = 4.75 KB per input channel. For bottleneck: [8, 1024, 4, 32] -> [8, 1024, 4, 32] Spatial size is only 4*32 = 128 pixels. For 1024 input and 1024 output channels, each pixel requires 1024*1024*9 = 9.4M MACs. For 128 pixels, that's 1.2B MACs total. A naive approach: each thread computes one output pixel for one output channel. That would be 128 * 1024 = 131,072 threads. We could launch 256 thread blocks of 512 threads each, where each thread computes one (b, c_out, h, w). For each thread: - Initialize accumulator to 0 - For c_in = 0 to 1023: - Read input values for 3x3 neighborhood (9 reads) - Read weights for this c_in, c_out (9 reads) - Compute 9 multiply-adds - Total reads per thread: 1024 * 9 * 2 = 18,432 float reads = 73.7 KB - At 3.35 TB/s global memory bandwidth, if perfectly coalesced, that's about 22 us. - But compute is 1.2B MACs = 2.4 GFLOPs. At 67 TFLOPS with all SMs, that's 36 us. Hmm, so for this layer, even a naive implementation might be memory-bandwidth limited, which means our conv performance could be close to optimal! The trick is coalescing. For a thread computing position (b, c_out, h, w): - Read input[b, c_in, h+dy, w+dx] for c_in from 0 to 1023 - These reads stride by channel, so adjacent threads (same c_out, different w or b) access adjacent memory if c_in is the slowest-changing dimension. In NCHW format, c_in is the channel dimension, so threads with different w access memory at offset w, which is adjacent! Good. Wait, in NCHW: input[b, c, h, w] -> memory offset = ((b * C + c) * H + h) * W + w So for fixed b, c, h, threads with adjacent w have adjacent memory addresses. Perfect coalescing along width. If we have a warp of 32 threads all computing the same (b, c_out, h) but w = 0..31: - They read input[b, c_in, h+dy, w+dx] for w=0..31 - These are 32 consecutive floats! Perfect coalescing. So actually, a very simple conv kernel might work well for the smaller spatial sizes! For larger spatial sizes like 64×512: - Threads per (b, c_out, h) = 512 width - That's exactly 16 warps of 32 threads. - 512 threads reading 512 consecutive floats. Perfect coalescing. So the naive approach of having each thread compute one output pixel might actually be surprisingly efficient if we coalesce well. But we need to process many output channels. 64 output channels * 64 height * 8 batch = 32,768 "rows" of 512 width pixels each. At 512 threads per block, we'd need 64 blocks. With 132 SMs on H100, that's fine. So here's a simple kernel design for 3x3 conv: - Each block has 512 threads (one per width pixel, or maybe fewer with each thread doing multiple widths) - Each block processes one (b, c_out, h) location - Thread i computes output[b, c_out, h, i] for i in its assigned widths - All threads in block iterate together over c_in from 0 to C_in-1 - For each c_in, they read 9 input values and 9 weights Wait, but this is extremely memory intensive. For each c_in, each thread does 9 input reads and 9 weight reads. For C_in = 64, that's 576 input reads + 576 weight reads per thread. That's 1536 floats per row, or 6144 floats total = 24.6 KB per c_in. - We load this for 64 channels: 64 * 24.6 KB = 1.54 MB. That's a lot to read repeatedly. If they load it together with coalescing, it's fast. - Load weights[c_out, c_in, :, :] = 9 floats. All threads need the same weights. These will be cached in L1. For the conv: ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3(...) { int w = threadIdx.x; // 0..511 int c_out = blockIdx.x; // or from combined index int h = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; float acc = 0; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ky++) { for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; kx++) { int ih = h + ky - 1; int iw = w + kx - 1; if (ih >= 0 && ih < H && iw >= 0 && iw < W) { float inp = input[((b * C_in + c_in) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + ky) * 3 + kx]; acc += inp * wgt; } } } } output[((b * C_out + c_out) * H + h) * W + w] = acc; } ``` Each thread does C_in * 9 iterations. All threads in a warp read adjacent iw values, so input accesses are coalesced. All threads read the same weight values, so weights are broadcast. This is extremely simple! For large spatial sizes, memory bandwidth might be the bottleneck, and coalescing will help. For small sizes, we might be fine too. The main issue: we read input 9 times per c_in. Each pixel in the 3x3 neighborhood is read multiple times across adjacent threads. But with cache, it might be OK. Wait, actually adjacent threads along width read adjacent iw values. Let me write out the kernel: ```cuda template<int TILE_W> __global__ void conv3x3_bn_softmax( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { extern __shared__ float smem[]; int w = threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (w >= W) return; // Compute conv float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ky++) { int ih = h + ky - 1; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; kx++) { int iw = w + kx - 1; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = input[((b * C_in + c_in) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 3 + ky) * 3 + kx]; acc += val * wgt; } } } acc += bias[c_out]; // Apply batch norm float mean = bn_mean[c_out]; float var = bn_var[c_out]; float gamma = bn_weight[c_out]; float beta = bn_bias[c_out]; float bn_val = (acc - mean) / sqrtf(var + 1e-5f) * gamma + beta; // Store in shared memory for softmax smem[w] = bn_val; __syncthreads(); // Softmax over width // Find max ... } ``` Wait, but if we use shared memory for softmax reduction, the size is W floats. Hmm, we can't easily change block size at runtime for the same kernel. But we CAN call cudaOccupancyMaxPotentialBlockSize or just launch with different block sizes. For simplicity, let me write the kernel to accept any block size: ```cuda __global__ void kernel(...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = B * C_out * H * W; for (int linear = idx; linear < total; linear += gridDim.x * blockDim.x) { // decode linear to b, c, h, w int w = linear % W; int tmp = linear / W; int h = tmp % H; tmp /= H; int c = tmp % C_out; int b = tmp / C_out; // compute conv for this pixel ... } } ``` This grid-stride loop approach is very flexible! We can launch with any block size and grid size. The downside: adjacent threads might not process adjacent (h, w) if W and H vary, but in NCHW format, width is the fastest-changing dimension. If we iterate linearly, adjacent linear IDs have adjacent w (unless w wraps around). When w wraps (every W pixels), h increments. For conv3x3, when adjacent threads have adjacent w, they read adjacent memory -> coalesced! When w wraps and h increments, adjacent threads read the next row -> memory is still contiguous if H*W is... wait, memory layout is [b, c, h, w] so (h, w) are contiguous. Adjacent h with w=0 is contiguous with previous h's w=W-1. Actually no, adjacent rows are contiguous in memory (row-major). Wait, in NCHW, the offset is ((b*C + c)*H + h)*W + w. So for fixed b, c, h: addresses from w=0 to w=W-1 are contiguous. For h=0, w=W-1, the next address is h=1, w=0. So adjacent linear IDs ARE contiguous in memory! Perfect. With grid-stride loops: Thread 0 processes linear=0 -> (b=0, c=0, h=0, w=0) Thread 1 processes linear=1 -> (b=0, c=0, h=0, w=1) ... Thread W-1 processes linear=W-1 -> (b=0, c=0, h=0, w=W-1) Thread W processes linear=W -> (b=0, c=0, h=1, w=0) Adjacent threads in a warp process adjacent linear IDs, which map to adjacent memory addresses. Perfect coalescing! However, there's a catch: when iterating in the for-loop, thread 0 processes linear=0, then linear=N, then linear=2N, etc. where N = gridDim.x * blockDim.x. If N > warp_size, then after the first iteration, thread 0 processes linear=N, thread 1 processes linear=N+1, etc. These are still adjacent! So coalescing is preserved across all iterations. This is great! But for the softmax: after computing conv for one pixel, we need to do a reduction across width for each (b, c, h). With the grid-stride loop, threads in the same block might not all belong to the same (b, c, h)! To handle softmax, we need a different strategy for the reduction. Option 1: Separate kernel for softmax. This is what PyTorch does. Option 2: Cooperative groups. Threads from the same (b, c, h) need to reduce together. With grid-stride, they might be in different blocks. Actually, if we configure the grid so that N = total_pixels_grid and ensure all threads for one (b, c, h) are in the same block... That's equivalent to having blockDim.x = W or a divisor of W. So for the grid-stride approach to fuse with softmax, we'd need blockDim.x to be a multiple or exact match of the width groupings. Hmm, this is problematic. Let me instead use a fixed assignment where each block handles exactly one (b, c, h) and blockDim.x = W (if W <= 1024). CUDA allows up to 1024 threads per block. W ranges from 32 to 512. So we can set blockDim.x = W for all layers where W <= 1024. But W varies. In CUDA, block dimensions are set at kernel launch time. We can pass different blockDim for different layers! Yes, of course. So at each kernel launch, we set: - block = dim3(W, 1, 1) - grid = dim3(C_out, H, B) This works for all layers since W <= 512 < 1024. And we can use shared memory for softmax reduction. For W = 32, block has 32 threads. Might not be enough warps for full occupancy, but with many blocks (B * C_out * H), we can still saturate the GPU. Actually, for W=32 and bottleneck conv2 with 1024 output channels, 4 height, 8 batch: Grid = (1024, 4, 8) = 32,768 blocks of 32 threads each. 32,768 * 32 = 1,048,576 threads total. On H100 with 132 SMs, each SM gets ~7,936 threads if evenly distributed. With max 1536 threads per SM, that's about 5 blocks per SM. Not great occupancy, but with 32 threads each, we can have 48 warps per SM max, and we're getting ~7,936/32 = 248 warps per SM... wait, no. 32,768 blocks / 132 SMs = 248 blocks per SM. Each block has 32 threads = 1 warp. GPU can have at most 64 warps per SM on H100 (with 2048 threads max, but actually it's 64 warps per SM). So 248 blocks per SM means some SMs get 248 warps total, but can only execute 64 concurrently. The rest are queued. This is fine. Actually, the occupancy might be limited by registers. 32 threads with few registers is fine. Wait, each block only has 1 warp. The GPU schedules warps, not blocks. So each SM can run up to 64 warps concurrently. With 248 blocks/warps per SM, 64 run and the rest wait. Is 1 warp per block efficient? It might have some overhead, but for compute-heavy kernels it's often fine. Actually, 1 warp per block is not ideal on modern GPUs due to scheduling granularity. It might work though. For W=512 and C_out=64, H=64, B=8: Grid = (64, 64, 8) = 32,768 blocks of 512 threads each = 16 warps per block. 32,768 * 16 = 524,288 warps total. / 132 SMs = 3,972 warps per SM. Each SM can run 64 warps, so we're well saturated. So the variable block size per layer should work fine. **Kernel design:** For `conv3x3_bn_softmax`, with block=(W, 1, 1) and grid=(C_out, H, B): ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_bn_softmax( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int w = threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; float acc = 0.0f; int in_base_b_c = (b * C_in) * H * W; int wgt_base_c_out = c_out * C_in * 9; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { int in_base = in_base_b_c + c_in * H * W; int wgt_base = wgt_base_c_out + c_in * 9; // Load 3x3 neighborhood for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ky++) { int ih = h + ky - 1; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; int in_row_base = in_base + ih * W; int wgt_row_base = wgt_base + ky * 3; for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; kx++) { int iw = w + kx - 1; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = input[in_row_base + iw]; float wgt = weight[wgt_row_base + kx]; acc += val * wgt; } } } acc += bias[c_out]; // Batch norm float gamma = bn_weight[c_out]; float beta = bn_bias[c_out]; float mean = bn_mean[c_out]; float var = bn_var[c_out]; float bn_val = (acc - mean) * rsqrtf(var + 1e-5f) * gamma + beta; // Softmax in shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; smem[w] = bn_val; __syncthreads(); // Reduction for max float local_max = bn_val; for (int offset = W / 2; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { if (w + offset < W) { local_max = fmaxf(local_max, smem[w + offset]); } __syncthreads(); if (offset > 1) { // Only need to sync if we continue // Actually, tree reduction needs careful sync } } ``` Wait, the tree reduction with shared memory requires a specific pattern. Let me write it correctly: ```cuda // Shared memory reduction for max int tid = threadIdx.x; for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (tid < offset && tid + offset < W) { smem[tid] = fmaxf(smem[tid], smem[tid + offset]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_val = smem[0]; __syncthreads(); // Compute exp(x - max) float exp_val = expf(bn_val - max_val); smem[tid] = exp_val; __syncthreads(); // Reduction for sum for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (tid < offset && tid + offset < W) { smem[tid] += smem[tid + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } float sum_val = smem[0]; // Normalize float out_val = exp_val / sum_val; output[((b * C_out + c_out) * H + h) * W + w] = out_val; ``` This reduction requires W to be a power of 2 for the tree to work cleanly. Our W values: 512=2^9, 256=2^8, 128=2^7, 64=2^6, 32=2^5. All powers of 2! Perfect. But wait, W=512 and blockDim.x=512 means we need 512 * 4 = 2KB shared memory. Shared memory size is auto-determined from the launch. Actually, I realize there's a problem: in the first tree reduction, we overwrite smem[tid] with the max. Then we need the original values for the exp computation. We should store the bn_val in a local variable, which we already have. Let me rewrite more carefully: ```cuda extern __shared__ float smem[]; float bn_val = ...; smem[tid] = bn_val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { smem[tid] = fmaxf(smem[tid], smem[tid + s]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_val = smem[0]; float exp_val = expf(bn_val - max_val); smem[tid] = exp_val; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { smem[tid] += smem[tid + s]; } __syncthreads(); } float sum_val = smem[0]; output[...] = exp_val / sum_val; ``` Wait, there's a bug: after the first reduction, smem[0] is the max. But the tree reduction modifies all entries. The values at `tid >= blockDim.x/2` might be modified. Then we write exp_val back, which is fine. But what about `bn_val`? Each thread still has its local `bn_val` variable. Good. However, there's an issue with numerical stability and performance. The reduction loops need `__syncthreads()` after every iteration. With 512 threads and 9 iterations, that's 9 syncs per softmax. For 2 softmaxes, 18 syncs per DoubleConv. Also, all threads in a warp execute the same path since `tid < s` is a continuous range. No warp divergence beyond the boundary when W < blockDim.x, but since W is always a power of 2 and blockDim.x = W, all threads participate equally. Wait, but if W < blockDim.x? We ensure blockDim.x = W exactly, so that's fine. One more thing: we need to sync after writing max_val so that all threads see it before computing exp. Since we read `smem[0]` after a `__syncthreads()`, and the last write to smem[0] was followed by `__syncthreads()`, it should be visible. But we need one more sync before the exp computation to ensure all threads have read smem[0]. Actually: ```cuda for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (tid < s) { smem[tid] = fmaxf(smem[tid], smem[tid + s]); } __syncthreads(); } float max_val = smem[0]; __syncthreads(); // Ensure all threads have read max_val ``` Yes, we need that extra sync. Actually, `smem[0]` is written by thread 0 in the last iteration (when s=1, thread 0 writes smem[0] = max(smem[0], smem[1])). Then we sync. After the sync, all threads can safely read smem[0]. Since there's a sync at the end of the for loop (when s=1, after thread 0 writes, we sync because the loop body ends with __syncthreads()), do we need another sync? Let's trace: for s=1: - tid=0: smem[0] = max(smem[0], smem[1]) - __syncthreads() After the loop, all threads read smem[0]. Since __syncthreads() ensures all threads have reached this point and all shared memory writes are visible, we don't need an extra sync. Great. Actually wait. When s=1, thread 0 writes to smem[0]. Then __syncthreads() makes it visible to all threads in the block. The loop then exits. Then `float max_val = smem[0];` reads the updated value. Since __syncthreads() was called before reading, it's fine. So no extra sync needed. However, there's another issue: the reduction modifies shared memory in place. When computing the sum, we need to make sure we don't read stale values. But since we overwrite all of shared memory with `exp_val` before the sum reduction, that's fine. One more optimization: we can use warp-level primitives (shfl) for the reduction when blockDim.x <= 32. But for simplicity, the tree reduction in shared memory works for all sizes. For blockDim.x = 32, shared memory reduction is fine but we might also use warp shuffles for the last 5 reductions. Actually, with 32 threads (1 warp), `__syncthreads()` is effectively a warp barrier, but using warp shuffles would be faster. However, for simplicity, I'll stick with shared memory for all sizes. Wait, actually if blockDim.x = 32, there's only 1 warp. The `__syncthreads()` inside a single warp is free-ish (just a __syncwarp()). So it's fine. **For the transposed conv (upconv):** ConvTranspose2d with kernel=2, stride=2. For output (h, w), input is at (h/2, w/2) and we use weight[c_out, c_in, h%2, w%2]. The kernel is simple: ```cuda __global__ void upconv2x2( const float* __restrict__ input, const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_in, int C_out, int H_in, int W_in, int H_out, int W_out ) { int w = threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; if (w >= W_out) return; int ih = h / 2; int iw = w / 2; int ky = h % 2; int kx = w % 2; float acc = 0.0f; for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in; c_in++) { float val = input[((b * C_in + c_in) * H_in + ih) * W_in + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_out * C_in + c_in) * 2 + ky) * 2 + kx]; acc += val * wgt; } acc += bias[c_out]; output[((b * C_out + c_out) * H_out + h) * W_out + w] = acc; } ``` Wait, for transposed conv, is this the correct interpretation? Let me check PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d with kernel=2, stride=2. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d(k=2, s=2) doubles the spatial size. For output at (h, w), the corresponding input is at (floor(h/2), floor(w/2)) because stride=2. The kernel offset is (h % 2, w % 2). Actually, I need to verify this. In transposed conv, each input pixel at (ih, iw) contributes to a 2x2 block in the output starting at (ih*2, iw*2). The weights for that block are weight[:, :, 0:2, 0:2]. So for output (h, w): - ih = h / 2 (integer division) - iw = w / 2 - ky = h % 2 - kx = w % 2 Yes, that's correct. And we use weight[c_out, c_in, ky, kx]. **For the concatenation conv:** In the decoder, we need to conv over two concatenated feature maps. Instead of literally concatenating, we can pass two input pointers and have the conv read from both. ```cuda __global__ void conv3x3_2input_bn_softmax( const float* __restrict__ input1, const float* __restrict__ input2, int C_in1, int C_in2, // C_in_total = C_in1 + C_in2 const float* __restrict__ weight, const float* __restrict__ bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_weight, const float* __restrict__ bn_bias, const float* __restrict__ bn_mean, const float* __restrict__ bn_var, float* __restrict__ output, int B, int C_out, int H, int W ) { int w = threadIdx.x; int c_out = blockIdx.x; int h = blockIdx.y; int b = blockIdx.z; float acc = 0.0f; int C_in_total = C_in1 + C_in2; // First input for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in1; c_in++) { for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ky++) { int ih = h + ky - 1; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; kx++) { int iw = w + kx - 1; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = input1[((b * C_in1 + c_in) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_out * C_in_total + c_in) * 3 + ky) * 3 + kx]; acc += val * wgt; } } } // Second input for (int c_in = 0; c_in < C_in2; c_in++) { for (int ky = 0; ky < 3; ky++) { int ih = h + ky - 1; if (ih < 0 || ih >= H) continue; for (int kx = 0; kx < 3; kx++) { int iw = w + kx - 1; if (iw < 0 || iw >= W) continue; float val = input2[((b * C_in2 + c_in) * H + ih) * W + iw]; float wgt = weight[((c_out * C_in_total + C_in1 + c_in) * 3 + ky) * 3 + kx]; acc += val * wgt; } } } acc += bias[c_out]; // BN + softmax... } ``` Hmm, for the weight indexing, the weight tensor should have shape [C_out, C_in_total, 3, 3]. reasoning
The issue is that the CUDA kernels are in a .cu file while the launch functions are in the C++ file. When using load_inline, the cuda_sources contains kernel definitions and the cpp_sources contains host-side wrapper definitions. But the C++ file doesn't know about dim3 or the kernels. I need to put the launch code (with <<< >>>) in the .cu file. Actually, looking at how load_inline works, it puts both together. But the issue is that the cpp_sources is compiled first with g++ which doesn't understand CUDA syntax. I should put everything (declarations + host wrappers) in cuda_sources, and only the forward declarations in cpp_sources. Or better: put only the forward declarations in cpp_sources and the actual kernel + launch code in cuda_sources. The cpp_sources should just be C++ compatible. The cuda_sources is compiled with nvcc and can contain both device and host code. So: - cpp_sources: C++ function declarations (no <<< >>>) - cuda_sources: kernels + host launch functions (with <<< >>>) Then functions=[...] should list the names that pybind11 will wrap, which are the functions declared in cpp_sources but defined in cuda_sources. Wait, but the issue is that load_inline requires the functions to be declared in cpp_sources and defined somewhere. Let me think about how load_inline organizes files. Actually, from the PyTorch docs: - `cpp_sources`: C++ source code (compiled with standard C++ compiler) - `cuda_sources`: CUDA source code (compiled with nvcc) The resulting shared library links both together. So I can declare functions in cpp_sources and define them in cuda_sources. BUT the issue is that CUDA syntax (<<< >>>) is only valid in .cu files. So I should: 1. In cpp_sources: only C++ declarations (no CUDA syntax) 2. In cuda_sources: kernel definitions + host wrappers using <<< >>> Then the linker will connect them. But actually, when I declared launch functions in cpp_sources AND defined them there too, the launch functions used CUDA syntax which isn't valid in a .cpp file compiled by g++. The right approach is: - cpp_sources: C++ declarations for the functions (and any other C++ code) - cuda_sources: kernel definitions + host wrappers using <<< >>> OR, much simpler: just put everything in cuda_sources and leave cpp_sources empty/minimal.
